<li id="cite_note-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Longitudinal_SystRev_dementia-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Longitudinal_SystRev_dementia_72-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFBlondellHammersley-MatherVeerman2014">Blondell SJ, Hammersley-Mather R, Veerman JL (May 2014). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4064273" rel="nofollow">"Does physical activity prevent cognitive decline and dementia?: A systematic review and meta-analysis of longitudinal studies"</a>. <i>BMC Public Health</i>. <b>14</b>: 510. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2458-14-510" rel="nofollow">10.1186/1471-2458-14-510</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4064273" rel="nofollow">4064273</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24885250" rel="nofollow">24885250</a>. <q>Longitudinal observational studies show an association between higher levels of physical activity and a reduced risk of cognitive decline and dementia. A case can be made for a causal interpretation. Future research should use objective measures of physical activity, adjust for the full range of confounders and have adequate follow-up length. Ideally, randomised controlled trials will be conducted. ... On the whole the results do, however, lend support to the notion of a causal relationship between physical activity, cognitive decline and dementia, according to the established criteria for causal inference.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=BMC+Public+Health&amp;rft.atitle=Does+physical+activity+prevent+cognitive+decline+and+dementia%3F%3A+A+systematic+review+and+meta-analysis+of+longitudinal+studies&amp;rft.volume=14&amp;rft.pages=510&amp;rft.date=2014-05&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4064273%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F24885250&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1186%2F1471-2458-14-510&amp;rft.aulast=Blondell&amp;rft.aufirst=SJ&amp;rft.au=Hammersley-Mather%2C+R&amp;rft.au=Veerman%2C+JL&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4064273&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Addiction_review_2016-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Addiction_review_2016_73-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFCarrollSmethells2016">Carroll ME, Smethells JR (February 2016). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4745113" rel="nofollow">"Sex Differences in Behavioral Dyscontrol: Role in Drug Addiction and Novel Treatments"</a>. <i>Front. Psychiatry</i>. <b>6</b>: 175. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3389%2Ffpsyt.2015.00175" rel="nofollow">10.3389/fpsyt.2015.00175</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4745113" rel="nofollow">4745113</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26903885" rel="nofollow">26903885</a>. <q>There is accelerating evidence that physical exercise is a useful treatment for preventing and reducing drug addiction ... In some individuals, exercise has its own rewarding effects, and a behavioral economic interaction may occur, such that physical and social rewards of exercise can substitute for the rewarding effects of drug abuse. ... The value of this form of treatment for drug addiction in laboratory animals and humans is that exercise, if it can substitute for the rewarding effects of drugs, could be self-maintained over an extended period of time. Work to date in [laboratory animals and humans] regarding exercise as a treatment for drug addiction supports this hypothesis. ... However, a <abbr title="randomized controlled trial">RTC</abbr> study was recently reported by Rawson et al., whereby they used 8 weeks of exercise as a post-residential treatment for METH addiction, showed a significant reduction in use (confirmed by urine screens) in participants who had been using meth 18 days or less a month. ... <a href="/wiki/Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise" title="Neurobiological effects of physical exercise">Animal and human research on physical exercise as a treatment for stimulant addiction indicates that this is one of the most promising treatments on the horizon.</a> [emphasis added]</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Front.+Psychiatry&amp;rft.atitle=Sex+Differences+in+Behavioral+Dyscontrol%3A+Role+in+Drug+Addiction+and+Novel+Treatments&amp;rft.volume=6&amp;rft.pages=175&amp;rft.date=2016-02&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4745113%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26903885&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3389%2Ffpsyt.2015.00175&amp;rft.aulast=Carroll&amp;rft.aufirst=ME&amp;rft.au=Smethells%2C+JR&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4745113&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Running_vs_addiction-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Running_vs_addiction_74-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Neurosci+Biobehav+Rev&amp;rft.atitle=Exercise+as+a+novel+treatment+for+drug+addiction%3A+a+neurobiological+and+stage-dependent+hypothesis&amp;rft.volume=37&amp;rft.issue=8&amp;rft.pages=1622-1644&amp;rft.date=2013-09&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3788047%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F23806439&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.neubiorev.2013.06.011&amp;rft.aulast=Lynch&amp;rft.aufirst=WJ&amp;rft.au=Peterson%2C+AB&amp;rft.au=Sanchez%2C+V&amp;rft.au=Abel%2C+J&amp;rft.au=Smith%2C+MA&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3788047&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Natural_and_drug_addictions-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Natural_and_drug_addictions_75-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFOlsen2011">Olsen CM (December 2011). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3139704" rel="nofollow">"Natural rewards, neuroplasticity, and non-drug addictions"</a>. <i>Neuropharmacology</i>. <b>61</b> (7): 1109–1122. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.neuropharm.2011.03.010" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.neuropharm.2011.03.010</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3139704" rel="nofollow">3139704</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/21459101" rel="nofollow">21459101</a>. <q>Similar to environmental enrichment, studies have found that exercise reduces self-administration and relapse to drugs of abuse (Cosgrove et al., 2002; Zlebnik et al., 2010). There is also some evidence that these preclinical findings translate to human populations, as exercise reduces withdrawal symptoms and relapse in abstinent smokers (Daniel et al., 2006; Prochaska et al., 2008), and one drug recovery program has seen success in participants that train for and compete in a marathon as part of the program (Butler, 2005). ... In humans, the role of dopamine signaling in incentive-sensitization processes has recently been highlighted by the observation of a dopamine dysregulation syndrome in some patients taking dopaminergic drugs. This syndrome is characterized by a medication-induced increase in (or compulsive) engagement in non-drug rewards such as gambling, shopping, or sex (Evans et al., 2006; Aiken, 2007; Lader, 2008).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Neuropharmacology&amp;rft.atitle=Natural+rewards%2C+neuroplasticity%2C+and+non-drug+addictions&amp;rft.volume=61&amp;rft.issue=7&amp;rft.pages=1109-1122&amp;rft.date=2011-12&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3139704%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F21459101&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.neuropharm.2011.03.010&amp;rft.aulast=Olsen&amp;rft.aufirst=CM&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3139704&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Exercise_Rev_3-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Exercise_Rev_3_76-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFLinkeUssher2015">Linke SE, Ussher M (2015). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4831948" rel="nofollow">"Exercise-based treatments for substance use disorders: evidence, theory, and practicality"</a>. <i>Am J Drug Alcohol Abuse</i>. <b>41</b> (1): 7–15. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3109%2F00952990.2014.976708" rel="nofollow">10.3109/00952990.2014.976708</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4831948" rel="nofollow">4831948</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25397661" rel="nofollow">25397661</a>. <q>The limited research conducted suggests that exercise may be an effective adjunctive treatment for SUDs. In contrast to the scarce intervention trials to date, a relative abundance of literature on the theoretical and practical reasons supporting the investigation of this topic has been published. ... numerous theoretical and practical reasons support exercise-based treatments for SUDs, including psychological, behavioral, neurobiological, nearly universal safety profile, and overall positive health effects.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Am+J+Drug+Alcohol+Abuse&amp;rft.atitle=Exercise-based+treatments+for+substance+use+disorders%3A+evidence%2C+theory%2C+and+practicality&amp;rft.volume=41&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=7-15&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4831948%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F25397661&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3109%2F00952990.2014.976708&amp;rft.aulast=Linke&amp;rft.aufirst=SE&amp;rft.au=Ussher%2C+M&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4831948&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Exercise,_addiction_prevention,_and_ΔFosB-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Exercise,_addiction_prevention,_and_ΔFosB_77-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFZhouZhaoZhouLi2015">Zhou Y, Zhao M, Zhou C, Li R (July 2015). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4712120" rel="nofollow">"Sex differences in drug addiction and response to exercise intervention: From human to animal studies"</a>. <i>Front. Neuroendocrinol</i>. <b>40</b>: 24–41. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.yfrne.2015.07.001" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.yfrne.2015.07.001</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4712120" rel="nofollow">4712120</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26182835" rel="nofollow">26182835</a>. <q>Collectively, these findings demonstrate that exercise may serve as a substitute or competition for drug abuse by changing ΔFosB or cFos immunoreactivity in the reward system to protect against later or previous drug use. ... As briefly reviewed above, a large number of human and rodent studies clearly show that there are sex differences in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_addiction" title="Drug addiction">drug addiction</a> and exercise. The sex differences are also found in the effectiveness of exercise on drug addiction prevention and treatment, as well as underlying neurobiological mechanisms. The postulate that exercise serves as an ideal intervention for drug addiction has been widely recognized and used in human and animal rehabilitation. ... In particular, more studies on the neurobiological mechanism of exercise and its roles in preventing and treating drug addiction are needed.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Front.+Neuroendocrinol.&amp;rft.atitle=Sex+differences+in+drug+addiction+and+response+to+exercise+intervention%3A+From+human+to+animal+studies&amp;rft.volume=40&amp;rft.pages=24-41&amp;rft.date=2015-07&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4712120%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26182835&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.yfrne.2015.07.001&amp;rft.aulast=Zhou&amp;rft.aufirst=Y&amp;rft.au=Zhao%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Zhou%2C+C&amp;rft.au=Li%2C+R&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4712120&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Neurooncology-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Neurooncology_78-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFCormieNowakChambersGalvão2015">Cormie P, Nowak AK, Chambers SK, Galvão DA, Newton RU (April 2015). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4389372" rel="nofollow">"The potential role of exercise in neuro-oncology"</a>. <i>Front. Oncol</i>. <b>5</b>: 85. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3389%2Ffonc.2015.00085" rel="nofollow">10.3389/fonc.2015.00085</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4389372" rel="nofollow">4389372</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25905043" rel="nofollow">25905043</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Front.+Oncol.&amp;rft.atitle=The+potential+role+of+exercise+in+neuro-oncology&amp;rft.volume=5&amp;rft.pages=85&amp;rft.date=2015-04&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4389372%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F25905043&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3389%2Ffonc.2015.00085&amp;rft.aulast=Cormie&amp;rft.aufirst=P&amp;rft.au=Nowak%2C+AK&amp;rft.au=Chambers%2C+SK&amp;rft.au=Galv%C3%A3o%2C+DA&amp;rft.au=Newton%2C+RU&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4389372&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Cochrane_exercise_depression-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Cochrane_exercise_depression_79-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise_Cochrane_exercise_depression_79-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFCooneyDwanGreigLawlor2013">Cooney GM, Dwan K, Greig CA, Lawlor DA, Rimer J, Waugh FR, McMurdo M, Mead GE (September 2013). "Exercise for depression". <i>Cochrane Database Syst. Rev</i>. <b>9</b> (9): CD004366. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2F14651858.CD004366.pub6" rel="nofollow">10.1002/14651858.CD004366.pub6</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24026850" rel="nofollow">24026850</a>. <q>Exercise is moderately more effective than a control intervention for reducing symptoms of depression, but analysis of methodologically robust trials only shows a smaller effect in favour of exercise. When compared to psychological or pharmacological therapies, exercise appears to be no more effective, though this conclusion is based on a few small trials.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Cochrane+Database+Syst.+Rev.&amp;rft.atitle=Exercise+for+depression&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.issue=9&amp;rft.pages=CD004366&amp;rft.date=2013-09&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2F14651858.CD004366.pub6&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F24026850&amp;rft.aulast=Cooney&amp;rft.aufirst=GM&amp;rft.au=Dwan%2C+K&amp;rft.au=Greig%2C+CA&amp;rft.au=Lawlor%2C+DA&amp;rft.au=Rimer%2C+J&amp;rft.au=Waugh%2C+FR&amp;rft.au=McMurdo%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Mead%2C+GE&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Pharmacodynamics_and_pharmacokinetics_of_HMB-CA_in_humans_in_vivo-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Pharmacodynamics_and_pharmacokinetics_of_HMB-CA_in_humans_in_vivo_98-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pharmacodynamics_and_pharmacokinetics_of_HMB-CA_in_humans_in_vivo_98-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFWilkinsonHossainLimbPhillips2018">Wilkinson DJ, Hossain T, Limb MC, Phillips BE, Lund J, Williams JP,  et al. (December 2018). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6295980" rel="nofollow">"Impact of the calcium form of β-hydroxy-β-methylbutyrate upon human skeletal muscle protein metabolism"</a>. <i>Clinical Nutrition</i>. <b>37</b> (6 Pt A): 2068–2075. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.clnu.2017.09.024" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.clnu.2017.09.024</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6295980" rel="nofollow">6295980</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29097038" rel="nofollow">29097038</a>. <q>Ca-HMB led a significant and rapid (&lt;60 min) peak in plasma HMB concentrations (483.6 ± 14.2 μM, p &lt; 0.0001). This rise in plasma HMB was accompanied by increases in MPS (PA: 0.046 ± 0.004%/h, CaHMB: 0.072 ± 0.004%/h, p &lt; [0.001]) and suppressions in MPB (PA: 7.6 ± 1.2 μmol Phe per leg min<sup>−1</sup>, Ca-HMB: 5.2 ± 0.8 μmol Phe per leg min<sup>−1</sup>, p &lt; 0.01). ... During the first 2.5 h period we gathered postabsorptive/fasted measurements, the volunteers then consumed 3.42 g of Ca-HMB (equivalent to 2.74 g of FA-HMB) ... It may seem difficult for one to reconcile that acute provision of CaHMB, in the absence of exogenous nutrition (i.e. EAA's) and following an overnight fast, is still able to elicit a robust, perhaps near maximal stimulation of MPS, i.e. raising the question as to where the additional AA's substrates required for supporting this MPS response are coming from. It would appear that the AA's to support this response are derived from endogenous intracellular/plasma pools and/or protein breakdown (which will increase in fasted periods). ... To conclude, a large single oral dose (~3 g) of Ca-HMB robustly (near maximally) stimulates skeletal muscle anabolism, in the absence of additional nutrient intake; the anabolic effects of Ca-HMB are equivalent to FA-HMB, despite purported differences in bioavailability (Fig. 4).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Clinical+Nutrition&amp;rft.atitle=Impact+of+the+calcium+form+of+%CE%B2-hydroxy-%CE%B2-methylbutyrate+upon+human+skeletal+muscle+protein+metabolism&amp;rft.volume=37&amp;rft.issue=6+Pt+A&amp;rft.pages=2068-2075&amp;rft.date=2018-12&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC6295980%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F29097038&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.clnu.2017.09.024&amp;rft.aulast=Wilkinson&amp;rft.aufirst=DJ&amp;rft.au=Hossain%2C+T&amp;rft.au=Limb%2C+MC&amp;rft.au=Phillips%2C+BE&amp;rft.au=Lund%2C+J&amp;rft.au=Williams%2C+JP&amp;rft.au=Brook%2C+MS&amp;rft.au=Cegielski%2C+J&amp;rft.au=Philp%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Ashcroft%2C+S&amp;rft.au=Rathmacher%2C+JA&amp;rft.au=Szewczyk%2C+NJ&amp;rft.au=Smith%2C+K&amp;rft.au=Atherton%2C+PJ&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC6295980&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Exercise_–_neurotrophic_factors_+_basal_ganglia-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Exercise_–_neurotrophic_factors_+_basal_ganglia_120-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFPaillardRollandde_Souto_Barreto2015">Paillard T, Rolland Y, de Souto Barreto P (July 2015). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4507374" rel="nofollow">"Protective Effects of Physical Exercise in Alzheimer's Disease and Parkinson's Disease: A Narrative Review"</a>. <i>Journal of Clinical Neurology</i>. <b>11</b> (3): 212–219. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3988%2Fjcn.2015.11.3.212" rel="nofollow">10.3988/jcn.2015.11.3.212</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4507374" rel="nofollow">4507374</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26174783" rel="nofollow">26174783</a>. <q>Aerobic physical exercise (PE) activates the release of neurotrophic factors and promotes angiogenesis, thereby facilitating neurogenesis and synaptogenesis, which in turn improve memory and cognitive functions. ... Exercise limits the alteration in dopaminergic neurons in the substantia nigra and contributes to optimal functioning of the basal ganglia involved in motor commands and control by adaptive mechanisms involving dopamine and glutamate neurotransmission.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Clinical+Neurology&amp;rft.atitle=Protective+Effects+of+Physical+Exercise+in+Alzheimer%27s+Disease+and+Parkinson%27s+Disease%3A+A+Narrative+Review&amp;rft.volume=11&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=212-219&amp;rft.date=2015-07&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4507374%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26174783&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3988%2Fjcn.2015.11.3.212&amp;rft.aulast=Paillard&amp;rft.aufirst=T&amp;rft.au=Rolland%2C+Y&amp;rft.au=de+Souto+Barreto%2C+P&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4507374&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-BDNF_meta_analysis-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-BDNF_meta_analysis_121-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFSzuhanyBugattiOtto2015">Szuhany KL, Bugatti M, Otto MW (January 2015). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4314337" rel="nofollow">"A meta-analytic review of the effects of exercise on brain-derived neurotrophic factor"</a>. <i>Journal of Psychiatric Research</i>. <b>60</b>: 56–64. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.jpsychires.2014.10.003" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.jpsychires.2014.10.003</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4314337" rel="nofollow">4314337</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25455510" rel="nofollow">25455510</a>. <q>Consistent evidence indicates that exercise improves cognition and mood, with preliminary evidence suggesting that brain-derived neurotrophic factor (BDNF) may mediate these effects. The aim of the current meta-analysis was to provide an estimate of the strength of the association between exercise and increased BDNF levels in humans across multiple exercise paradigms. We conducted a meta-analysis of 29 studies (N = 1111 participants) examining the effect of exercise on BDNF levels in three exercise paradigms: (1) a single session of exercise, (2) a session of exercise following a program of regular exercise, and (3) resting BDNF levels following a program of regular exercise. Moderators of this effect were also examined. Results demonstrated a moderate effect size for increases in BDNF following a single session of exercise (Hedges' g = 0.46, p &lt; 0.001). Further, regular exercise intensified the effect of a session of exercise on BDNF levels (Hedges' g = 0.59, p = 0.02). Finally, results indicated a small effect of regular exercise on resting BDNF levels (Hedges' g = 0.27, p = 0.005). ... Effect size analysis supports the role of exercise as a strategy for enhancing BDNF activity in humans</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Psychiatric+Research&amp;rft.atitle=A+meta-analytic+review+of+the+effects+of+exercise+on+brain-derived+neurotrophic+factor&amp;rft.volume=60&amp;rft.pages=56-64&amp;rft.date=2015-01&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4314337%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F25455510&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.jpsychires.2014.10.003&amp;rft.aulast=Szuhany&amp;rft.aufirst=KL&amp;rft.au=Bugatti%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Otto%2C+MW&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4314337&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-VEGF_aging_brain-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-VEGF_aging_brain_122-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFBouchardVilleda2015">Bouchard J, Villeda SA (January 2015). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4301186" rel="nofollow">"Aging and brain rejuvenation as systemic events"</a>. <i>Journal of Neurochemistry</i>. <b>132</b> (1): 5–19. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fjnc.12969" rel="nofollow">10.1111/jnc.12969</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4301186" rel="nofollow">4301186</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25327899" rel="nofollow">25327899</a>. <q>From a molecular perspective, elevated systemic levels of circulating growth factors such as vascular endothelial growth factor and insulin-like growth factor 1 (IGF-1) in blood elicited by increased exercise have been shown to mediate, in part, enhancements in neurogenesis (Trejo et al. 2001; Fabel et al. 2003).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Neurochemistry&amp;rft.atitle=Aging+and+brain+rejuvenation+as+systemic+events&amp;rft.volume=132&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=5-19&amp;rft.date=2015-01&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4301186%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F25327899&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Fjnc.12969&amp;rft.aulast=Bouchard&amp;rft.aufirst=J&amp;rft.au=Villeda%2C+SA&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4301186&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Fitness_mechanism-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Fitness_mechanism_123-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFSilvermanDeuster2014">Silverman MN, Deuster PA (October 2014). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4142018" rel="nofollow">"Biological mechanisms underlying the role of physical fitness in health and resilience"</a>. <i>Interface Focus</i>. <b>4</b> (5): 20140040. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1098%2Frsfs.2014.0040" rel="nofollow">10.1098/rsfs.2014.0040</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4142018" rel="nofollow">4142018</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25285199" rel="nofollow">25285199</a>. <q>Importantly, physical exercise can improve growth factor signalling directly or indirectly by reducing pro-inflammatory signalling…. Exercise-induced increases in brain monoamines (norepinephrine and serotonin) may also contribute to increased expression of hippocampal BDNF [194]. In addition, other growth factors—insulin-like growth factor-1 (IGF-1) and vascular endothelial growth factor—have been shown to play an important role in BDNF-induced effects on neuroplasticity …, as well as exerting neuroprotective effects of their own …, thereby contributing to the beneficial effects of exercise on brain health.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Interface+Focus&amp;rft.atitle=Biological+mechanisms+underlying+the+role+of+physical+fitness+in+health+and+resilience&amp;rft.volume=4&amp;rft.issue=5&amp;rft.pages=20140040&amp;rft.date=2014-10&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4142018%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F25285199&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1098%2Frsfs.2014.0040&amp;rft.aulast=Silverman&amp;rft.aufirst=MN&amp;rft.au=Deuster%2C+PA&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4142018&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Comprehensive_review_–_repeat_citation-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Comprehensive_review_–_repeat_citation_124-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFGomez-PinillaHillman2013">Gomez-Pinilla F, Hillman C (January 2013). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3951958" rel="nofollow">"The influence of exercise on cognitive abilities"</a>. <i>Comprehensive Physiology</i>. <b>3</b> (1): 403–428. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fcphy.c110063" rel="nofollow">10.1002/cphy.c110063</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-470-65071-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-470-65071-4"><bdi>978-0-470-65071-4</bdi></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3951958" rel="nofollow">3951958</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23720292" rel="nofollow">23720292</a>. <q>Abundant research in the last decade has shown that exercise is one of the strongest promoters of neurogenesis in the brain of adult rodents … and humans …, and this has introduced the possibility that proliferating neurons could contribute to the cognitive enhancement observed with exercise. In addition to BDNF, the actions of IGF-1 and vascular endothelial growth factor (VEGF) (54) are considered essential for the angiogenic and neurogenic effects of exercise in the brain. Although the action of exercise on brain angiogenesis has been known for many years (10), it is not until recently that neurovascular adaptations in the hippocampus have been associated with cognitive function (29). Exercise enhances the proliferation of brain endothelial cells throughout the brain (113), hippocampal IGF gene expression (47), and serum levels of both IGF (178) and VEGF (63). IGF-1 and VEGF, apparently produced in the periphery, support exercise induced neurogenesis and angiogenesis, as corroborated by blocking the effects of exercise using antibodies against IGF-1 (47) or VEGF (63).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Comprehensive+Physiology&amp;rft.atitle=The+influence+of+exercise+on+cognitive+abilities&amp;rft.volume=3&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=403-428&amp;rft.date=2013-01&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3951958%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F23720292&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2Fcphy.c110063&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-470-65071-4&amp;rft.aulast=Gomez-Pinilla&amp;rft.aufirst=F&amp;rft.au=Hillman%2C+C&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3951958&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Cerebral_hemodynamics_and_AD-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Cerebral_hemodynamics_and_AD_125-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFTarumiZhang2014">Tarumi T, Zhang R (January 2014). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3896879" rel="nofollow">"Cerebral hemodynamics of the aging brain: risk of Alzheimer disease and benefit of aerobic exercise"</a>. <i>Frontiers in Physiology</i>. <b>5</b>: 6. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3389%2Ffphys.2014.00006" rel="nofollow">10.3389/fphys.2014.00006</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3896879" rel="nofollow">3896879</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24478719" rel="nofollow">24478719</a>. <q>Exercise-related improvements in brain function and structure may be conferred by the concurrent adaptations in vascular function and structure. Aerobic exercise increases the peripheral levels of growth factors (e.g., BDNF, IFG-1, and VEGF) which cross the blood-brain barrier (BBB) and stimulate neurogenesis and angiogenesis (Trejo et al., 2001; Lee et al., 2002; Fabel et al., 2003; Lopez-Lopez et al., 2004). Consistent with this, exercise-related enlargement of hippocampus was accompanied by increases in cerebral blood volume and capillary densities (Pereira et al., 2007). Enhanced cerebral perfusion may not only facilitate the delivery of energy substrates, but also lower the risk of vascular-related brain damages, including WMH and silent infarct (Tseng et al., 2013). Furthermore, regular aerobic exercise is associated with lower levels of Aβ deposition in individuals with APOE4 positive (Head et al., 2012), which may also reduce the risk of cerebral amyloid angiopathy and microbleeds (Poels et al., 2010).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Frontiers+in+Physiology&amp;rft.atitle=Cerebral+hemodynamics+of+the+aging+brain%3A+risk+of+Alzheimer+disease+and+benefit+of+aerobic+exercise&amp;rft.volume=5&amp;rft.pages=6&amp;rft.date=2014-01&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3896879%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F24478719&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3389%2Ffphys.2014.00006&amp;rft.aulast=Tarumi&amp;rft.aufirst=T&amp;rft.au=Zhang%2C+R&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3896879&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExercise"></span></span>
</li>

"Exercise for improving balance in older people". <i>The Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews</i>.
